Zakat — Almsgiving

Intention for Zakat: Timing and Exceptions in Hanafi Fiqh
Detailed explanation of intention as a condition for valid zakat payment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on when intention is required for zakat payment and apply the exceptions for setting aside, full charity, and agency.

Core Idea
Intention at setting aside suffices – hardship of repeated intentions removed.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains that while intention must normally accompany the act of worship, zakat payment has special flexibility. The commentator elaborates on the matn's conditions, showing how the Hanafi school balances principle with practical ease.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Zakat payment is wajib; intention is a condition of its validity (shart al-sihhah), not of obligation.
Intention distinguishes zakat from other payments.
2
Locate the primary exception
Because payment may be spread out, intention at the time of setting aside ('azl) suffices to remove hardship.
This is a concession (rukhsah) from the general rule of concurrent intention.
3
Understand constructive intention
If one pays without intention but later intends while the wealth is still in the recipient's hand, it is valid. This is called hukmi (constructive) conjunction.
Contrast with intention after the wealth is destroyed – then it is invalid.
4
Analyze the agency case
If the owner appoints an agent and intends when handing the money to the agent, the agent's subsequent payment without intention is valid.
The principal's intention is credited because he is the real payer.
5
Examine the full charity exception
If the entire nisab is given as charity, no specific intention is needed because the full payment itself clearly indicates zakat.
The conflict (muzahamah) is removed, so the purpose of intention is fulfilled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (وشرط أدائها نية مقارنة للأداء ولعزل ما وجب أو تصدق بكله بيان لشرط الصحة فإن شرائطها ثلاثة أنواع : شرائط وجوب وهي ما ذكره إلا لحول فإنه من شروط الأداء وجوب بدليل جواز التعجيل قبله بعد وجود السبب، وأما النية فهي شرط الصحة لكل عبادة كما قدمناه وقد علمت من قوله أولاً لله تعالى لكن المراد هنا بيان تفاصيلها. والأصل اقترانها بالأداء كسائر العبادات إلا أن الدفع يتفرق فيحرج باستحضار النية عند كل دفع فاكتفى بوجودها حالة العزل دفعاً للحرج. وإنما سقطت عنه بلا نية فيما إذا تصدق النصاب لأن الواجب جزء منه وقد وصل إلى مستحقه وإنما تشترط النية لدفع المزاحم فلما أدى الكل زالت المزاحمة أطلق المقارنة فشمل المقارنة الحقيقية وهو ظاهر، والحكمية كما إذا دفع بلا نية ثم حضرته النية والمال قائم في يد الفقير فإنه يجزئه وهو بخلاف ما إذا نوى بعد هلاكه، وكما إذا وكل رجلاً بدفع زكاة ماله ونوى المالك عند الدفع إلى الوكيل فدفع الوكيل بلا نية فإنه يجزئه لأن المعتبر نية الآمر لأنه المؤدي حقيقة ولو دفعها إلى ذمي ليدفعها إلى
His statement: 'And the condition for its performance is an intention concurrent with the performance, or with setting aside what became obligatory, or giving all of it in charity' is an explanation of the condition of validity. For its conditions are three types: Conditions of obligation, which are what he mentioned, except the year, for it is a condition of the obligation of performance, as evidenced by the permissibility of advancing it before the year after the cause exists. As for intention, it is a condition of validity for every worship, as we have previously mentioned, and you have known from his statement 'for Allah the Exalted,' but the intent here is to elaborate its details. The principle is its conjunction with performance like other acts of worship, except that payment may be spread out, making it difficult to bring intention at each payment, so the existence of intention at the time of setting aside suffices to remove hardship. And it is dropped from him without intention in the case where he gives the entire nisab in charity, because the obligatory is a part of it and it has reached its entitled recipient. Intention is only required to remove the conflict of interest, so when he pays all, the conflict is removed. He made the conjunction absolute, so it includes real conjunction, which is apparent, and constructive conjunction, such as when he pays without intention, then intention occurs while the wealth is still in the hand of the poor person: it suffices. This is unlike if he intends after its destruction. And also if he appoints a man to pay his zakat and the owner intends at the time of payment to the agent, and the agent pays without intention, it suffices because the intention of the commander is considered, as he is the one actually paying. And if he pays it to a dhimmi to pay it to
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage details the requirement of intention (niyyah) for the validity of zakat payment. The matn states that intention must accompany the act of payment, or the act of setting aside the zakat, or if one gives the entire nisab as charity. The commentator explains that while intention is normally required concurrent with the act, an exception is made for zakat because the payment may be dispersed; thus intention at the time of segregating the zakat money ('azl) suffices. Cases are discussed: if one gives the entire nisab, no specific intention is needed because the full payment clearly designates the zakat. A constructive intention is valid: if one pays without intention but later intends while the money is still with the recipient, the zakat is valid. Similarly, if a person appoints an agent and the owner intends at the time of handing to the agent, the agent's subsequent payment without intention is valid because the principal's intention counts. The text ends with an incomplete sentence about paying to a dhimmi.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): The intention that determines the act as zakat and distinguishes it from charity or other payments.
عزل ('azl): Setting aside the zakat money from the rest of the wealth.
أداء (ada'): The performance or payment of the zakat obligation.
شرط صحة (shart al-sihhah): A condition without which the act is invalid though the obligation remains.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which case is the intention requirement dropped entirely for zakat payment?
A) When the zakat is paid through an agent.
B) When the entire nisab is given as charity.
C) When the payment is made in installments.
D) When the creditor is a dhimmi.

Key Takeaway
Intention for zakat can be made at the time of setting aside, and the Hanafi school accommodates practical difficulty in repeated payments.

Intention in Zakat: Conditions, Relaxations, and the Agent's Reimbursement Dispute
Commentary on the condition of intention for zakat payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on intention for zakat payment, identify the three exceptions to the requirement of concomitant intention, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the agent's right to reclaim payment.

Core Idea
Intention for zakat is normally required at payment, but the Hanafi school relaxes this to setting aside or giving all nisab as charity.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks a key rule: intention is a condition for the validity of zakat payment. While the principle demands intention at the time of payment, the law provides reliefs to avoid hardship. The commentary then explores several applications, including constructive intention and agency, culminating in a dispute between the two great disciples of Abu Hanifa—a dispute with practical consequences for anyone acting as an agent in zakat distribution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
Begin by locating the core ruling: intention is ‘shart al-siha’ for zakat payment. Note the default requirement that intention must accompany the act of payment, just as in other acts of worship.
This is the baseline; the rest of the passage explains modifications.
2
Understand the relaxation via setting aside (al-‘azl)
Because zakat may be paid in installments, requiring intention at each payment would be burdensome. The law permits intention to be made at the time the money is set aside, and that intention covers subsequent payments.
This is a clear application of the principle of removing hardship (raf‘ al-haraj).
3
Analyse the exception when the entire nisab is given as charity
If a person gives away the whole nisab as charity without any specific zakat intention, the obligation is still fulfilled. Reason: the obligation is a fraction of that amount, and by giving all, the need to distinguish zakat from voluntary charity disappears.
The absence of intention is cured because there is no conflicting ownership that intention would remove.
4
Work through the agency scenarios
The passage discusses three agency situations: (1) owner intends when handing money to agent – valid; (2) owner pays a dhimmī to distribute – valid; (3) paying another’s zakat without order then getting approval – invalid. Understand the principle: the actual payer is the one with authority and intention.
Note the distinction between valid and invalid agency.
5
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on agent's recourse
Abu Yusuf analogizes the agent's payment to debt payment, allowing recourse without stipulation. Muhammad requires stipulation. Consider the 'illa in each view and the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).
This dispute does not affect the validity of the zakat; it concerns who bears the financial burden.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (وشرط أدائها نية مقارنة للأداء ولعزل ما وجب أو تصدق بكله بيان لشرط الصحة فإن شرائطها ثلاثة أنواع : شرائط وجوب وهي ما ذكره إلا لحول فإنه من شروط الأداء وجوب بدليل جواز التعجيل قبله بعد وجود السبب، وأما النية فهي شرط الصحة لكل عبادة كما قدمناه وقد علمت من قوله أولاً لله تعالى لكن المراد هنا بيان تفاصيلها. والأصل اقترانها بالأداء كسائر العبادات إلا أن الدفع يتفرق فيحرج باستحضار النية عند كل دفع فاكتفى بوجودها حالة العزل دفعاً للحرج. وإنما سقطت عنه بلا نية فيما إذا تصدق النصاب لأن الواجب جزء منه وقد وصل إلى مستحقه وإنما تشترط النية لدفع المزاحم فلما أدى الكل زالت المزاحمة أطلق المقارنة فشمل المقارنة الحقيقية وهو ظاهر، والحكمية كما إذا دفع بلا نية ثم حضرته النية والمال قائم في يد الفقير فإنه يجزئه وهو بخلاف ما إذا نوى بعد هلاكه، وكما إذا وكل رجلاً بدفع زكاة ماله ونوى المالك عند الدفع إلى الوكيل فدفع الوكيل بلا نية فإنه يجزئه لأن المعتبر نية الآمر لأنه المؤدي حقيقة ولو دفعها إلى ذمي ليدفعها إلى فقراء جاز لوجود النية من الآمر ولو أدى زكاة غيره بغير أمره فبلغه فأجاز لم يجز لأنها نفاذاً على المتصدق لأنها ملكه ولم يصر نائباً عن غيره فنفذت عليه. ولو تصدق عنه بأمره جاز ويرجع بما دفع عند أبي يوسف وإن لم يشترط الرجوع كالأمر بقضاء الدين، وعند محمد لا رجوع له إلا بالشرط. وتمامه في الخانية : ولو أعطاه دراهم ليتصدق بها تطوعاً فلم يتصدق بها حتى نوى الأمر أن تكون زكاته ثم تصدق بها أجزأه وكذا لو قال تصدق بها عن كفارة يميني ثم نوى عن زكاة ماله
His statement: (And the condition for its performance is intention concomitant to the performance, or to setting aside what is obligatory, or giving all of it as charity) is an explanation of the condition of validity. For its conditions are three types: conditions of obligation, which he mentioned except the year, for it is a condition of performance obligation evidenced by the permissibility of advance payment after the cause exists. As for intention, it is a condition of validity for every act of worship as we have stated, and you have known from his statement first 'for Allah Almighty', but the intended meaning here is to elaborate its details. The principle is its accompaniment of performance as in other acts of worship, except that payment is distributed, so it would be difficult to renew intention at each payment, so it is deemed sufficient to have it at the time of setting aside to remove hardship. It is only dropped without intention when one gives the entire nisab as charity, because the obligatory amount is a part of it and has reached its rightful recipient; intention is only required to remove the conflicting [ownership], so when he gave all, the conflict is removed. He applied 'concomitance' absolutely, so it includes real concomitance, which is apparent, and constructive concomitance, as when one pays without intention then the intention comes to him while the wealth is still in the hand of the poor person, then it suffices, unlike if he intends after its destruction. Similarly, if he appoints a man to pay the zakat of his wealth and the owner intends at the time of payment to the agent, then the agent pays without intention, it suffices because the commander's intention is considered, for he is the actual payer. If he pays it to a dhimmī to give it to the poor, it is valid because of the intention from the commander. If he pays the zakat of another without his order, and news reaches him and he approves, it is not valid because it is effective upon the charitable giver since it is his property, and he did not become a deputy for another, so it is effective upon him. If he gives charity on his behalf with his order, it is valid, and he can reclaim what he paid according to Abu Yusuf even if he did not stipulate recourse, like the case of paying a debt. According to Muhammad, he has no recourse except with stipulation. The completion of it is in al-Khāniyya: If someone gives him dirhams to give as voluntary charity, and he did not give them until the commander intended them to be his zakat, then he gives them as charity, it suffices. Similarly, if he said: 'Give these as expiation for my oath,' then he intended them for his zakat.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the condition of intention for zakat payment. The author explains that intention is a condition of validity (shart al-siha) for zakat payment. While in principle intention must accompany the act of payment, the law eases this due to hardship when payment is made in multiple installments. Therefore, intention at the time of setting aside (al-'azl) suffices. Moreover, if one gives the entire nisab as charity without explicit intention, it fulfills zakat because the obligation is a portion of that amount and has reached the deserving. Intention serves to distinguish zakat from voluntary charity; when all is given, the need for distinction disappears. The passage also discusses constructive intention: if intention arises after payment while the property is still with the recipient, it is valid. Agency is also covered: the owner's intention at the time of giving to the agent suffices, even if the agent pays without intention. A key intra-Hanafi dispute is noted between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the agent's right to reclaim payment made on behalf of the principal: Abu Yusuf allows recourse even without stipulation (by analogy to debt payment), while Muhammad requires a stipulation.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Intention required for the validity of zakat payment; it distinguishes zakat from voluntary charity.
عزل ('azl): Setting aside a portion of one's wealth specifically for zakat, with or without immediate payment.
وكيل (wakīl): Agent appointed to disburse zakat; the owner's intention when handing over the money to the agent is sufficient.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is zakat considered valid even if the payer did not intend at the exact moment of payment?
A) Only if the obligation is paid in a single lump sum.
B) When the entire nisab is given as charity without any specific intention.
C) When the recipient is a close relative.
D) When the payment is made before the year ends.

Key Takeaway
Intention is a condition for zakat validity, but the Hanafi school provides two major relaxations: intention at setting aside suffices, and giving the entire nisab as charity fulfills the obligation without explicit intention.

Zakat Agency: Mixing, Payment, and Delinquency
Rulings on mixing, liability, valid payment, and delinquency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the liability rules for agents who mix zakat funds, identify when payment to a collector is valid, and explain the legal consequences of delaying zakat payment.

Core Idea
Merely setting aside zakat does not discharge the obligation; actual delivery to the poor is required.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Rāʾiq presents a series of practical rulings on zakat that every agent, collector, and payer should know. It addresses when an agent becomes personally liable for mixing funds, how to ensure payment to a collector is valid, and what happens if the payer delays or dies. Understanding these rules protects both the poor's rights and the payer's fulfilment of obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling on mixing
Read the first part where two men give zakat to an agent who mixes them. Determine when the agent becomes liable and when he is excused (customary permission).
Liability arises from unauthorized mixing; the exception is the miller by custom.
2
Examine payment to a collector (jābī)
Analyze the conditions: if the collector is the poor's agent, payment is valid only if the giver knows the funds reached nisab. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad also allow payment without knowledge if the collector is not the poor's agent.
This involves a known disagreement among the Hanafi imams.
3
Understand the effect of setting aside (ʿazl)
Focus on the ruling that merely separating zakat funds does not absolve the payer; loss falls on the payer until delivery. Also note the exception for the state collector (sāʿī).
The hand of the sāʿī is like the hand of the poor.
4
Analyze the poor's rights and refusal to pay
Review that the poor cannot take zakat forcibly; the payer may reclaim if taken unlawfully. The state collector may imprison but not take by force.
Coercion invalidates zakat because intention (niyya) is compromised.
5
Consider the case of death and bequest
If the payer dies, zakat is not taken from the estate without a will, as intention is absent. If bequeathed, it is treated like other donations from one-third.
Zakat is an act of worship requiring intention at payment.

وفي الفتاوى رجلان دفع كل واحد منهما زكاة ماله إلى رجل ليؤدي عنه فخلط مالهما ثم تصدق ضمن الوكيل، وكذا لو كان في يد رجل أوقاف مختلفة فخلط إنزال الأوقاف، وكذلك البياع والسمسار والطحان إلا في موضع يكون الطحان مأذوناً بالخلط عرفاً .انتهى. وبه يعلم حكم من يجمع للفقراء ومحله ما إذا لم يوكلوه فإن كان من جانب الفقراء أيضاً فلا ضمان عليه، فإذا ضمن في صورة الخلط لا تسقط الزكاة عن أربابها، فإذا أدى صار مؤدياً مال نفسه. كذا في التجنيس ولو لم يخلط الجابي فإنه يجوز دفع من أعطى قبل أن تبلغ الدراهم مائتين ولا يجوز لمن أعطى بعدما بلغت نصاباً إن كان الفقير وكل الجابي وعلم المعطى ببلوغه نصاباً، فإن لم يكن الجابي وكيل الفقير جاز مطلقاً، وإن لم يعلم المعطى ببلوغه نصاباً جاز في قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد. كذا في الظهيرية. وللوكيل بدفع الزكاة أن يدفعها إلى ولد نفسه كبيراً كان أو صغيراً وإلى امرأته إذا كانوا محاويج ولا يجوز أن يمسك لنفسه شيئاً اهـ . إلا إذا قال ضعها حيث شئت فله أن يمسكها لنفسه. كذا في الولوالجية. وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لا يخرج بعزل ما وجب عن العهدة بل لا بد من الأداء إلى الفقير لما في الخانية : لو أفرز من النصاب خمسة ثم ضاعت لا تسقط عنه الزكاة. ولو مات بعد إفرازها كانت الخمسة ميراثاً عنه .اهـ. بخلاف إذا ضاعت في يد الساعي لأن يده كيد الفقراء. كذا في المحيط . وفي التجنيس : لو عزل الرجل زكاة ماله ووضعه في ناحية من بيته فسرقها منه سارق لم تقطع يده للشبهة، وقد ذكر في كتاب السرقة من هذا الكتاب أنه يقطع السارق غنياً كان أو فقيراً اه بلفظه، وإلى أنه لو أخر الزكاة ليس للفقير أن يطالبه ولا أن يأخذ ماله بغير علمه وإن أخذ كان لصاحب المال أن . يسترده إن كان قائماً ويضمنه إن كان هالكاً، فإن لم يكن في قرابة من عليه الزكاة أو في قبيلته أحوج من هذا الرجل فكذلك ليس له أن يأخذها له، وإن أخذ كان ضامناً في الحكم، أما فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى يرجى أن يحل له الأخذ كذا في الخانية أيضاً. وإلى أنه لو مات من عليه الزكاة لا تؤخذ من تركته لفقد شرط صحتها وهو النية إلا إذا أوصى بها فتعتبر من الثلث كسائر التبرعات وإلى أنه لو امتنع من أدائها فالساعي لا يأخذ منه كرهاً، ولو أخذ لا يقع عن الزكاة لكونها بلا اختيار ولكن يجبره بالحبس ليؤدي بنفسه لأن الإكراه لا يسلب الاختيار بل الطواعية فيتحقق الأداء عن اختيار . كذا في المحيط . وفي مختصر الطحاوي ومن امتنع عن أداء زكاة ماله
And in al-Fatāwā: Two men each gave their zakat wealth to a man to pay on their behalf, then he mixed their wealth and then gave charity; the agent is liable. Similarly, if in the hands of a man there are various endowments and he mixes the proceeds of the endowments, likewise. And similarly the seller, the broker, and the miller, except where the miller is customarily permitted to mix. End. And by this is known the ruling of one who collects for the poor; its place is when they did not appoint him. If it is from the side of the poor as well, then there is no liability upon him. If he becomes liable in the case of mixing, the zakat is not dropped from its owners; if he pays, he becomes a payer of his own wealth. Thus in al-Tajnīs. And if the collector does not mix, then it is permissible to pay from what was given before the dirhams reach two hundred [dirhams], and not permissible for one who gave after it reached a nisāb if the poor person had appointed the collector and the giver knew it reached a nisāb. If the collector is not the agent of the poor, it is permissible absolutely. And if the giver did not know it reached a nisāb, it is permissible according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Thus in al-Ẓahīriyya. And the agent for paying zakat may pay it to his own child, whether major or minor, and to his wife, if they are in need; and he may not keep anything for himself. End. Except if he said 'Place it where you wish', then he may keep it for himself. Thus in al-Walwālijiyya. And the author indicated that one does not exit liability by setting aside what is obligatory; rather, payment to the poor is necessary. Because in al-Khāniyya: If he separates five [dirhams] from the nisāb and they are lost, the zakat is not dropped from him. And if he dies after separating them, the five are inheritance from him. End. Contrary to if it is lost in the hands of the collector, because his hand is like the hand of the poor. Thus in al-Muḥīṭ. And in al-Tajnīs: If a man sets aside his zakat and places it in a corner of his house and a thief steals it, the thief's hand is not cut due to the [ownership] doubt. And it was mentioned in the Book of Theft of this book that the thief's hand is cut whether rich or poor. End verbatim. And that if he delays the zakat, the poor person may not demand it nor take his wealth without his knowledge; if he takes it, the owner may reclaim it if it still exists and may hold him liable if it is destroyed. If there is in the kinship of the one upon whom zakat is due, or in his tribe, one more needy than this man, then likewise he may not take it for him, and if he takes it, he is liable in the judgment. As for between him and Allah Almighty, it is hoped that it is permissible for him to take. Thus in al-Khāniyya as well. And that if the one upon whom zakat is due dies, it is not taken from his estate due to the absence of its condition of validity, which is intention, unless he bequeathed it, then it is considered from the third like other donations. And that if he refuses to pay it, the collector may not take it forcefully; if he does take it, it does not count as zakat because it is without choice. But he compels him by imprisonment to pay himself, because coercion does not negate choice but rather [negates] willingness, so payment occurs by choice. Thus in al-Muḥīṭ. And in Mukhtaṣar al-Ṭaḥāwī: And whoever refuses to pay his zakat...
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage presents a collection of rulings on practical issues related to zakat payment. First, it discusses the liability of an agent who mixes zakat funds from different owners; mixing creates personal liability, and the zakat obligation is not discharged from the original owners until actual payment reaches the poor. Exceptions are made for those customarily permitted to mix (e.g., millers by custom). Another ruling concerns the collector (jābī): if he does not mix funds, payment before reaching nisab is permissible, but after reaching nisab, the giver must ensure the poor person has appointed the collector. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that if the giver is unaware of the nisab threshold, payment is valid. The agent may pay zakat to his own needy family members but not to himself unless given broad discretion. Importantly, merely setting aside (ʿazl) does not remove liability; if the set-aside amount is lost, the payer remains obligated. If the collector loses it, it is considered lost from the poor due to his trusteeship. Regarding theft of set-aside zakat, the thief's hand is not cut due to doubt over ownership. A major point: the poor person cannot forcibly take zakat from a delinquent payer; if he takes without permission, the owner can reclaim it. Delaying payment does not give the poor a right to self-help. If the payer dies, zakat is not taken from the estate without a will, as intention is lacking. Finally, the state collector (sāʿī) cannot take zakat by force; he may imprison the payer to compel willing payment, as coercion under pressure still allows choice.

Key Terms
خلط (khalṭ): Mixing zakat funds from multiple owners by an agent, which creates personal liability unless custom permits.
جابي (jābī): Collector of zakat or other funds; his hand is like the hand of the poor, so loss in his custody falls on the poor.
ساعي (sāʿī): State-appointed zakat collector; cannot take zakat by force but may compel by imprisonment to ensure willing payment.
عزل (ʿazl): Setting aside a portion of wealth with the intention of paying zakat; does not discharge obligation until delivery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a person sets aside a portion of his wealth for zakat but dies before distributing it?
A) The set-aside amount is considered zakat paid and is not part of the estate.
B) The set-aside amount becomes part of his inheritance and zakat is not considered fulfilled.
C) The heirs must distribute it as zakat from the estate.
D) The zakat obligation is dropped because of death.

Key Takeaway
Zakat obligation is only fulfilled when the wealth reaches the poor or their authorized representative; mere segregation or delay does not suffice.

Rulings on the Zakat Agent (Wakil)
Rulings on the agent for zakat—validity, recovery, liability, and scope of authority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules regarding an agent appointed to pay zakat, including the effect of subsequent approval, the recovery of expenses, and the consequences of mixing funds.

Core Idea
An agent for zakat must follow the principal's instructions; mixing funds triggers liability and does not discharge zakat.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq details the legal framework for an agent (wakil) appointed to distribute zakat. It covers critical scenarios: whether the principal's subsequent approval validates an unauthorized payment, the dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on recovery of expenses, and the severe consequences of mixing zakat funds. Understanding these rules ensures that the zakat obligation is properly discharged.

Guided Path
1
Analyze unauthorized payment with subsequent approval
Determine the ruling when someone pays another's zakat without permission and the owner later approves. Note that in the Hanafi school, ijazah (subsequent approval) does not retroactively validate the act because the payment was not made as a deputy for the owner at the time.
This is based on the principle that a person cannot be made a representative after the fact.
2
Examine the recovery dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
Compare the two positions on whether an agent who pays zakat by command can recover the amount without stipulating repayment. Abu Yusuf analogizes to debt payment; Muhammad requires a stipulation. Recognize the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).
The text does not give a preferred view; later Hanafi works lean toward Abu Yusuf.
3
Identify the liability from mixing funds
When an agent mixes zakat money from different principals, he becomes liable for any loss or mispayment. The zakat of the principals is not discharged; the agent's payment is considered from his own wealth. Note exceptions for custom, e.g., millers.
Mixing without permission is akin to tadlis (breach of trust).
4
Understand the timing rules for collectors
If the collector does not mix, he may pay from collected amounts before the nisab is reached. After nisab, payment is valid only if the poor person appointed the collector and the giver knew the nisab was reached. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule that if the giver is unaware, payment is still valid.
This protects the giver's intention.
5
Apply the agent's discretion and restrictions
The agent may give zakat to his own needy children and wife, but cannot keep any for himself unless the principal gives him discretion (e.g., 'Place it where you wish').
This reflects the fiduciary nature of agency.

Source Text

وجدت وكيلاً الفقراء جاز لوجود النية من الآمر ولو أدى زكاة غيره بغير أمره فبلغه فأجاز لم يجز لأنها نفاذاً على المتصدق لأنها ملكه ولم يصر نائباً عن غيره فنفذت عليه. ولو تصدق عنه بأمره جاز ويرجع بما دفع عند أبي يوسف وإن لم يشترط الرجوع كالأمر بقضاء الدين، وعند محمد لا رجوع له إلا بالشرط. وتمامه في الخانية : ولو أعطاه دراهم ليتصدق بها تطوعاً فلم يتصدق بها حتى نوى الأمر أن تكون زكاته ثم تصدق بها أجزأه وكذا لو قال تصدق بها عن كفارة يميني ثم نوى عن زكاة ماله وفي الفتاوى رجلان دفع كل واحد منهما زكاة ماله إلى رجل ليؤدي عنه فخلط مالهما ثم تصدق ضمن الوكيل، وكذا لو كان في يد رجل أوقاف مختلفة فخلط إنزال الأوقاف، وكذلك البياع والسمسار والطحان إلا في موضع يكون الطحان مأذوناً بالخلط عرفاً .انتهى. وبه يعلم حكم من يجمع للفقراء ومحله ما إذا لم يوكلوه فإن كان من جانب الفقراء أيضاً فلا ضمان عليه، فإذا ضمن في صورة الخلط لا تسقط الزكاة عن أربابها، فإذا أدى صار مؤدياً مال نفسه. كذا في التجنيس ولو لم يخلط الجابي فإنه يجوز دفع من أعطى قبل أن تبلغ الدراهم مائتين ولا يجوز لمن أعطى بعدما بلغت نصاباً إن كان الفقير وكل الجابي وعلم المعطى ببلوغه نصاباً، فإن لم يكن الجابي وكيل الفقير جاز مطلقاً، وإن لم يعلم المعطى ببلوغه نصاباً جاز في قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد. كذا في الظهيرية. وللوكيل بدفع الزكاة أن يدفعها إلى ولد نفسه كبيراً كان أو صغيراً وإلى امرأته إذا كانوا محاويج ولا يجوز أن يمسك لنفسه شيئاً اهـ . إلا إذا قال ضعها حيث شئت فله أن يمسكها لنفسه. كذا في الولوالجية.
If you find an agent for the poor, it is valid because the intention is from the commander. If someone paid zakat for another without his command, then he heard and approved, it is not valid because it is a ratification for the giver since it is his property and he did not become a deputy for another, so it is binding on him. If he gave charity for him by his command, it is valid and he recovers what he paid according to Abu Yusuf even if he did not stipulate recovery, like the command to pay debt. According to Muhammad, there is no recovery except with stipulation. Its completion is in al-Khaniyya: If he gave him dirhams to give charity voluntarily and he did not give charity until the commander intended them to be his zakat, then he gave charity with them, it suffices. Similarly, if he said: Give charity from this for my oath expiation, then intended it for zakat of his wealth. And in al-Fatawa: Two men each gave their zakat to a man to pay for them, so he mixed their money then gave charity; the agent is liable. Similarly, if a man had different endowments in his hand and mixed the endowments. So too the seller, broker, and miller except where the miller is permitted to mix customarily. End. By this, the ruling is known for one who collects for the poor, and its place is when they do not appoint him; if also from the side of the poor, there is no liability. So if he becomes liable in the case of mixing, the zakat does not fall from its owners; so when he pays, he becomes one paying his own wealth. Likewise in al-Tajnīs. If the collector did not mix, then it is valid to pay for one who gave before the dirhams reach two hundred, and not valid for one who gave after they reached a nisab, if the poor person appointed the collector and the giver knew it reached a nisab. If the collector is not the agent of the poor, it is valid absolutely. If the giver did not know it reached a nisab, it is valid according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Likewise in al-Zahīriyya. The agent for paying zakat may give it to his own child, whether adult or minor, and to his wife if they are in need. He may not keep anything for himself. Except if he said: Place it where you wish, then he may keep it for himself. Likewise in al-Walwālijiyya.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings regarding the agent (wakil) appointed to distribute zakat. The author expands on various scenarios. First, if a person pays zakat on behalf of another without being asked, and the owner later approves, it is not valid because approval cannot retroactively validate an act done without permission; the act is attributed to the giver, not the owner. Conversely, if the owner expressly commands payment, it is valid, and the agent can recover the amount from the owner without stipulating recovery according to Abu Yusuf, analogizing to debt payment. Muhammad requires a stipulation. Then, if someone gives money to an agent for voluntary charity, but the agent delays and the principal intends it as zakat, it suffices because the intention can be changed before dispersal. Similarly, if the principal changes the purpose from kaffarah to zakat. The text then addresses mixing of funds: if two principals each give their zakat to the same agent and he mixes them, he is liable for any loss or mispayment, because mixing is akin to misappropriation. This applies to endowments, sellers, brokers, and millers except where custom permits mixing. The ruling also covers collection for the poor: if the agent collects without being appointed by the poor, he is not liable; but if he is also an agent for the poor, he is. If he becomes liable, the zakat obligation of the principals is not discharged; his payment is considered his own wealth. Regarding timing: if the collector does not mix, he can pay zakat from what he receives even before the collected amount reaches nisab, because each payment is separate. Once the total reaches nisab, further payments are not valid unless the giver knows the total and the poor person has appointed the collector. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule that if the giver is unaware of the total reaching nisab, the payment is still valid. Finally, the agent may give zakat to his own needy relatives (children, wife), but may not take any for himself unless the principal gives him discretion.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakīl): Agent appointed to perform an act (here, paying zakat) on behalf of the principal.
إجازة (ijāzah): Subsequent approval of an act done without permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling if an agent mixes zakat from two principals and then pays?
A) The payment is valid and zakat is discharged for both.
B) The agent is liable and the zakat of the principals is not discharged.
C) The payment is valid only if the poor consent.
D) The agent is not liable if the mixing was customary.

Key Takeaway
A zakat agent must act strictly within the principal's authority; mixing funds without permission incurs liability and does not discharge the zakat obligation.

Excuses and Effects of Zakat Payment: Loss, Death, Delay, and Ruler's Intervention
Rulings on excuses and effects in discharging zakat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on loss of set-aside zakat, death before delivery, delay in payment, and forcible collection by the ruler, and explain the reasoning behind the distinction between apparent and hidden wealth.

Core Idea
Setting aside zakat does not discharge liability; only delivery to the poor does, and the ruler's forcible taking suffices only for apparent wealth.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq addresses several critical issues related to discharging zakat. It clarifies that mere segregation is not enough, what happens if the money is lost or the owner dies, the rights of the poor to take without permission, and the role of the ruler in coercing payment. Understanding these rulings is essential for both the individual and the Islamic state in ensuring proper zakat fulfillment.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the ruling on loss of set-aside zakat
Identify that setting aside (ʿazl) does not remove liability; if the set-aside amount is lost, the obligation remains. The exception is if it is lost in the hands of the sāʿī, because his hand is like the hand of the poor.
This is based on the principle that liability persists until delivery to the rightful recipient.
2
Examine the consequence of death before delivery
If the obligated person dies after setting aside zakat, the set-aside amount becomes part of his estate and is inherited. Zakat is not taken from the estate because intention (niyya) is a condition for validity, which cannot be fulfilled after death unless he bequeathed it.
Bequest is treated as a voluntary gift from one-third.
3
Understand the poor's right to unilaterally take delayed zakat
The poor have no right to demand or take the zakat without the owner's permission. If they take it, the owner can reclaim it if it still exists or claim compensation if it is lost. However, between him and Allah, taking by extreme need may be excused.
Even relatives cannot take without permission.
4
Differentiate the ruler's authority over apparent vs. hidden wealth
The muftā bihi: If the ruler forcibly takes zakat from a reluctant payer, it discharges the obligation for apparent wealth (e.g., livestock, crops) because the ruler has authority to collect. For hidden wealth (e.g., gold, silver), the ruler has no such authority, so his taking does not discharge the obligation.
The issue arises because intention is a condition; the ruler's taking without the owner's intention is problematic, but for apparent wealth, the ruler's authority substitutes.
5
Note the issue of intention and coercion
If the ruler takes forcibly without the owner's consent, it does not count as zakat because intention is missing. However, the ruler may compel the owner by imprisonment to pay voluntarily, because coercion does not negate choice but rather volition.
The imam's taking stands in place of the owner's payment if he has authority, resolving the dilemma for apparent wealth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لا يخرج بعزل ما وجب عن العهدة بل لا بد من الأداء إلى الفقير لما في الخانية : لو أفرز من النصاب خمسة ثم ضاعت لا تسقط عنه الزكاة. ولو مات بعد إفرازها كانت الخمسة ميراثاً عنه .اهـ. بخلاف إذا ضاعت في يد الساعي لأن يده كيد الفقراء. كذا في المحيط . وفي التجنيس : لو عزل الرجل زكاة ماله ووضعه في ناحية من بيته فسرقها منه سارق لم تقطع يده للشبهة، وقد ذكر في كتاب السرقة من هذا الكتاب أنه يقطع السارق غنياً كان أو فقيراً اه بلفظه، وإلى أنه لو أخر الزكاة ليس للفقير أن يطالبه ولا أن يأخذ ماله بغير علمه وإن أخذ كان لصاحب المال أن يسترده إن كان قائماً ويضمنه إن كان هالكاً، فإن لم يكن في قرابة من عليه الزكاة أو في قبيلته أحوج من هذا الرجل فكذلك ليس له أن يأخذها له، وإن أخذ كان ضامناً في الحكم، أما فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى يرجى أن يحل له الأخذ كذا في الخانية أيضاً. وإلى أنه لو مات من عليه الزكاة لا تؤخذ من تركته لفقد شرط صحتها وهو النية إلا إذا أوصى بها فتعتبر من الثلث كسائر التبرعات وإلى أنه لو امتنع من أدائها فالساعي لا يأخذ منه كرهاً، ولو أخذ لا يقع عن الزكاة لكونها بلا اختيار ولكن يجبره بالحبس ليؤدي بنفسه لأن الإكراه لا يسلب الاختيار بل الطواعية فيتحقق الأداء عن اختيار . كذا في المحيط . وفي مختصر الطحاوي ومن امتنع عن أداء زكاة ماله : نأخذها من . وأخذها الإمام كرهاً منه فوضعها في أهلها أجزأه لأن للإمام ولاية أخذه الصدقات فقام أخذه مقام دفع المالك اهـ. وفي القنية : فيه إشكال لأن النية فيها شرط ولم توجد منه اهـ . وفي المجمع : ولا سائمة امتنع ربها من أدائها بغير رضاه بل نأمره ليؤديها اختياراً اهـ. والمفتى به التفصيل إن كان في الأموال الظاهر فإنه يسقط الفرض عن أربابها بأخذ السلطان أو نائبه لأن ولاية الأخذ له فبعد ذلك إن لم يضع السلطان موضعها لا يبطل أخذه عنه، وإن كان في الأموال الباطنة فإنه لا يسقط الفرض لأنه ليس للسلطان ولاية أخذ زكاة الأموال الباطنة فلم يصح أخذه . كذا في التجنيس والواقعات والولوالجية.
And the author indicated that by setting aside what is due, one does not exit from liability; rather delivery to the poor is necessary. As in al-Khāniyya: If he separated five (dirhams) from the nisab then they were lost, zakat is not remitted from him. If he died after setting them aside, the five are inheritance from him. End. Contrary to if they were lost in the hand of the collector (sā‘ī), because his hand is like the hand of the poor. Likewise in al-Muhīt. And in al-Tajnīs: If a man set aside his zakat and placed it in a corner of his house, and a thief stole it from him, his hand is not cut due to the ambiguity (shubha). And it has been mentioned in the Book of Theft in this book that the thief's hand is cut, whether rich or poor. End with his wording. And that if he delayed zakat, the poor person has no right to demand it or take his property without his knowledge; and if he took, the owner of the property may reclaim it if it still exists, and make him liable if it perished. If there is not among the relatives of the one obligated for zakat or in his tribe someone more needy than this man, likewise he may not take it for himself; and if he took, he is liable in legal judgment. As between him and Allah Almighty, it is hoped that taking is permissible for him. Likewise in al-Khāniyya. And that if the one obligated for zakat dies, it is not taken from his estate due to the absence of a condition of its validity, which is intention, unless he bequeathed it; then it is considered from one-third like other voluntary gifts. And that if he refuses to pay it, the sā‘ī does not take it from him by force; and if he takes, it does not count as zakat because it is without choice. But he compels him by imprisonment to pay himself, because coercion does not negate choice but rather volition, so payment occurs by choice. Likewise in al-Muhīt. And in Mukhtasar al-Tahāwī: And from one who refuses to pay zakat of his wealth: we take it from him. And if the imam took it from him forcibly and placed it among its people, it suffices because the imam has authority to take sadaqat, so his taking stands in place of the owner's payment. End. In al-Qunyah: There is a problem because intention is a condition and it did not exist from him. End. In al-Majma‘: And no grazing animal whose owner refuses to pay it without his consent; rather we order him to pay it by choice. End. The relied-upon (muftā bihi) is the detail: if it is concerning apparent wealth (amwāl ẓāhira), the obligation falls from its owners by the ruler or his deputy taking it, because he has authority to take; after that, if the ruler does not place it in its place, his taking does not nullify it for him. If it is concerning hidden wealth (amwāl bāṭina), then the obligation does not fall because the ruler has no authority to take zakat of hidden wealth, so his taking is not valid. Likewise in al-Tajnīs, al-Wāqi‘āt, and al-Walwālijiyya.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage addresses several issues regarding the discharge of zakat obligation. First, merely segregating the zakat amount does not remove liability; actual delivery to the poor is required. If the set-aside money is lost, the obligation remains; if the person dies, it becomes part of the estate. The exception is when the money is lost in the hands of the official collector (sā‘ī), whose hand is like the poor's hand, so the obligation is discharged. The text then discusses theft of set-aside zakat: the thief's hand is not cut because there is shubha (ambiguity) — it might be considered as belonging to the poor. However, in the Book of Theft, it says the thief's hand is cut regardless, which indicates a difference of opinion or context. Next, if a person delays payment, the poor cannot unilaterally take the money; if they do, the owner can reclaim it. This is even if the poor are relatives; they cannot take without permission, though it is hoped Allah will accept it if taken by extreme need. If the obligated person dies, zakat is not taken from his estate because intention (niyya) is a condition for validity, which cannot be fulfilled after death unless he bequeathed it, in which case it is treated as a voluntary bequest from one-third. Finally, the text examines the role of the ruler (sulṭān) in coercing payment. If the ruler forcibly takes zakat from a reluctant payer, does it discharge the obligation? There is detail: for apparent wealth (e.g., livestock, crops), the ruler has authority, so it suffices; for hidden wealth (e.g., gold, silver), the ruler has no such authority, so it does not discharge the obligation. The muftā bihi (relied-upon opinion) is this distinction.

Key Terms
ساعي (sā‘ī): Official collector of zakat appointed by the ruler.
شبهة (shubha): Legal ambiguity that suspends prescribed penalties (e.g., theft of zakat money).
عزل (ʿazl): Setting aside the exact amount of zakat from one's wealth.
نية (niyya): Intention, a condition for the validity of zakat.
أموال ظاهرة (amwāl ẓāhira): Apparent wealth visible to the public, e.g., livestock and crops.
أموال باطنة (amwāl bāṭina): Hidden wealth, e.g., gold, silver, cash.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The zakat obligation is discharged because the ruler has authority.
B) The zakat obligation is not discharged and the person must pay again.
C) The obligation is discharged only if the ruler distributes it to the poor.
D) The obligation is discharged but the person is rewarded for being compelled.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, zakat liability is only discharged by actual delivery to the poor or a legitimate agent; the ruler's forcible taking is effective only for apparent wealth due to his authority.

Partial Payment and Debt as Zakat: Intra-Hanafi Dispute and Legal Tricks
Rulings on partial payment and debt as zakat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on partial zakat payment and apply the rules for discharging zakat through debt forgiveness, including the legal trick (hīla).

Core Idea
Forgiving a poor person's debt counts as zakat; partial payment without intention only discharges that portion according to Imam Muhammad.

Zakat is a wajib obligation, but its discharge is not always straightforward. What if you pay only part of the nisab as charity without specifying zakat? Or if you forgive a debt owed to you by a poor person? This lesson explores the detailed Hanafi rules on these scenarios, including an intra-madhhab dispute between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf, and a permissible legal trick to combine zakat and debt repayment.

Guided Path
1
Partial payment of nisab without intention
Identify the scenario: a person pays part of his nisab (e.g., 200 dirhams) as sadaqah without intending zakat. All scholars agree the full zakat is not discharged. The dispute is whether zakat on the portion given falls.
Muhammad says yes; Abu Yusuf says no unless exactly 196 dirhams.
2
Debt forgiveness as zakat
When a person forgives a debt owed by a poor person, this counts as zakat whether or not the creditor explicitly intends zakat. The debt is considered 'ayn upon receipt.
Forgiving part of the debt discharges zakat of that part only.
3
The legal trick (hīla) for simultaneous discharge
The debtor (zakat payer) can give cash zakat to the poor person, then immediately take it back as repayment of a personal loan. This combines both obligations validly.
The poor person must be truly poor for the zakat to count.
4
Conditions and exceptions
The recipient need not know the payment is zakat; the payer's intention suffices. The debtor must be poor; forgiving a rich person's debt does not discharge zakat.
If the payer intends a different obligation (e.g., nadhr), zakat must still be paid separately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بالتصدق بالكل لأنه لو تصدق ببعض النصاب بلا نية اتفقوا أنه لا يسقط زكاة كله، واختلفوا في سقوط زكاة ما تصدق به فقال محمد بسقوطه، وقال أبو يوسف عليه زكاة كله إلا إذا كان الموهوب مائة وستة وتسعين فحينئذ تسقط . كذا في المبتغى بالغين المعجمة . وأطلق في التصدق بالكل فشمل العين والدين فلو كان له على فقير دين فأبرأه عنه سقط زكاته عنه ، نوى الزكاة أو لم ينو لما قدمناه ولو أبرأه عن البعض سقط زكاة ذلك البعض ولا تسقط عنه زكاة الباقي ولو نوى به الأداء عن الباقي لأن الباقي يصير عيناً بالقبض فيصير مؤدياً الدين عن العين والأصل فيه أن أداء العين عن العين وعن الدين يجوز، وأداء الدين عن العين وعن دين سيقبض لا يجوز، وأداء الدين عن دين لا يقبض يجوز . كذا في شرح الطحاوي. وحيلة الجواز أن يعطى المديون الفقير خمسة زكاة ثم يأخذها منه قضاء عن دينه . كذا في المحيط . ولو أمر فقيراً بقبض دين له على آخر نواه عن زكاة عين عنده جاز لأن الفقير يقبض عيناً فكان عيناً عن عين . كذا في الولوالجية . وقيدنا بكون من عليه الدين فقيراً لأنه لو كان غنياً فوهبه بعد الحول ففيه روايتان، أصحهما الضمان كما في المحيط وقد قدمناه . وشمل أيضاً ما إذا لم ينو شيئاً أصلاً أو نوى غير الزكاة وهو الصحيح فيما إذا نوى التطوع، أما إذا تصدق بكله ناوياً النذر أو واجباً آخر فإنه يقع عما نوى ويضمن قدر الواجب . كذا في التبيين . وفي شرح الطحاوي لو وجبت الزكاة في مائتي درهم فأدى خمسة ونوى ذلك تطوعاً سقطت عنه زكاة الخمسة وهي ثمن درهم ولا تسقط عنه زكاة الباقي اهـ. وينبغي أن يكون مفرعاً على قول محمد كما لا يخفى. ولم يشترط المصنف رحمه الله علم الآخذ بما يأخذه أنه زكاة للإشارة إلى أنه ليس بشرط وفيه اختلاف والأصح كما في المبتغى والقنية أن من أعطى مسكيناً دراهم وسماها هبة أو قرضاً ونوى الزكاة فإنها تجزئه، ولم يشترط أيضاً الدفع من عين
And he conditioned by giving charity with the whole, because if he gave charity with some of the nisab without intention, they agreed that the zakat of the whole does not fall, and they differed regarding the fall of zakat of what was given in charity: Muhammad said it falls, Abu Yusuf said upon him is zakat of the whole unless the gift was one hundred and ninety-six, then it falls. Likewise in al-Mubtaghā with ghayn. And he made absolute in giving charity with the whole so it includes ready money ('ayn) and debt. So if he had a debt upon a poor person and he discharges him from it, his zakat falls from him, whether he intended zakat or not, due to what we have presented. And if he discharged him from part, the zakat of that part falls, and the zakat of the remainder does not fall from him even if he intended thereby payment of the remainder, because the remainder becomes 'ayn upon receipt, so he becomes one paying debt from 'ayn. The principle is: payment of 'ayn for 'ayn and for debt is valid; payment of debt for 'ayn and for debt that will be received is not valid; payment of debt for debt that is not received is valid. Likewise in Sharh al-Tahāwī. The legal trick (hīla) for permissibility is that the debtor gives the poor person five (dirhams) as zakat, then takes it from him as payment for his debt. Likewise in al-Muhīt. And if he ordered a poor person to collect a debt owed to him from another, intending it as zakat from 'ayn he has, it is valid because the poor person collects 'ayn, so it is 'ayn for 'ayn. Likewise in al-Walwālijiyya. And we conditioned that the debtor be poor, because if he were rich and he grants him (the debt) after the year, there are two narrations; the more correct is liability, as in al-Muhīt, and we have presented it. And it also includes when he did not intend anything at all or intended other than zakat, and it is correct when he intended voluntary charity; but if he gave charity with the whole intending a vow or other obligation, it falls for what he intended and he must pay the amount of the obligation. Likewise in al-Tabyīn. And in Sharh al-Tahāwī: If zakat becomes obligatory on two hundred dirhams, and he pays five and intends it as voluntary charity, the zakat of the five—which is one-eighth dirham—falls, and the zakat of the remainder does not fall. End. It should be derived from the statement of Muhammad, as is not hidden. And the author (may Allah have mercy on him) did not stipulate that the taker knows what he takes is zakat, to indicate that it is not a condition. There is difference of opinion, and the most correct is as in al-Mubtaghā and al-Qunyah: that whoever gives dirhams to a poor person and calls it a gift or loan, but intends zakat, it suffices for him. He also did not stipulate payment from 'ayn.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage continues with detailed rules on partial payment of zakat and the use of debt as a means of discharging zakat. The author first discusses the case where a person gives part of the nisab in charity without specific intention for zakat. All agree that zakat on the entire nisab is not discharged. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Muhammad holds that zakat on the portion given is discharged; Abu Yusuf holds that the entire nisab remains subject to zakat unless the given portion is 196 dirhams (which is just below nisab?), then it falls. The text then moves to the case where one forgives a debt owed by a poor person; this counts as zakat, whether the creditor intended zakat or not, because the debt is akin to money that will be received. Forgiving part discharges zakat of that part. The principle is: paying 'ayn (cash) for 'ayn or debt is valid; paying debt for 'ayn is invalid; paying debt for an uncollectable debt is valid. A legal trick (hīla) is given: the debtor (zakat payer) gives cash zakat to the poor person, then immediately takes it back as payment of the personal debt, thus combining zakat and debt settlement. If the payer instructs a poor person to collect a debt from a third party, intending it as zakat from his cash, it is valid because the poor person receives cash. The ruling is conditioned on the debtor being poor; if rich, forgiving his debt after the year does not discharge zakat and the debtor must pay. Finally, the text clarifies that the recipient need not know the payment is zakat; the payer's intention suffices. If the payer gives the entire nisab as charity intending a different obligation, it counts for that obligation and he must still pay zakat.

Key Terms
حيلة (hīla): A legally permissible stratagem to achieve a desired result without violating the law.
عين (‘ayn): Tangible property, cash (as opposed to debt).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Zakat is discharged only if the total nisab is given entirely.
B) Zakat on 10 dirhams is discharged.
C) Zakat is not discharged because intention was made after the act.
D) Zakat on the entire wealth is discharged.

Key Takeaway
Partial forgiveness of a poor person's debt discharges zakat proportionally, but paying part of the nisab without intention only discharges that portion according to Imam Muhammad.

Imam's Forcible Zakat Taking: Apparent vs Hidden Wealth
Ruling on imam forcibly taking zakat and its effect on obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between apparent and hidden wealth regarding the imam's authority to take zakat forcibly, and state the mufta bihi position with its proof.

Core Idea
The imam's forcible taking of zakat only fulfills the obligation for apparent wealth, not hidden wealth.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq, we address a practical question: if a ruler forcibly takes zakat from an unwilling owner, does it satisfy the religious duty? The answer depends on the type of asset. The Hanafi school relies on the imam's authority (wilaya) over visible wealth, but for hidden assets the owner's consent and intention remain essential. Let's unpack the ruling, its proof, and the relied-upon fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal question
Determine what the passage is ruling on: the validity of the imam's forcible taking of zakat and whether it discharges the owner's obligation.
2
Recognize the key distinction
Note the two categories: apparent wealth (amwal zahira) and hidden wealth (amwal batina). The ruling differs for each.
Apparent wealth includes livestock and crops; hidden includes gold, silver, and trade goods.
3
Examine the proof for apparent wealth
The imam has authority (wilaya) to collect zakat on apparent wealth. His taking stands in place of the owner's payment, so the obligation is fulfilled even if the owner did not intend.
Rational proof (dalil aqli): the imam's authority substitutes for the owner's act.
4
Address the objection about intention
Al-Quniyya raises the issue that intention (niyya) is a condition, yet it is absent here. The response is that the imam's authority overrides the need for the owner's intention for apparent wealth.
The fatwa does not follow that objection; it upholds the tafsīl.
5
State the mufta bihi and its application
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the detail: for apparent wealth, the imam's taking suffices even if he misallocates funds; for hidden wealth, the ruler has no authority, so his taking does not discharge the owner's duty.
Sources: al-Tajnis, al-Waqi'at, al-Walwaljiyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأخذها الإمام كرهاً منه فوضعها في أهلها أجزأه لأن للإمام ولاية أخذه الصدقات فقام أخذه مقام دفع المالك اهـ. وفي القنية : فيه إشكال لأن النية فيها شرط ولم توجد منه اهـ . وفي المجمع : ولا سائمة امتنع ربها من أدائها بغير رضاه بل نأمره ليؤديها اختياراً اهـ. والمفتى به التفصيل إن كان في الأموال الظاهر فإنه يسقط الفرض عن أربابها بأخذ السلطان أو نائبه لأن ولاية الأخذ له فبعد ذلك إن لم يضع السلطان موضعها لا يبطل أخذه عنه، وإن كان في الأموال الباطنة فإنه لا يسقط الفرض لأنه ليس للسلطان ولاية أخذ زكاة الأموال الباطنة فلم يصح أخذه . كذا في التجنيس والواقعات والولوالجية .
And if the imam takes it [the zakat] forcibly from him and places it among its recipients, it suffices him, because the imam has authority to take the sadaqat, so his taking stands in place of the owner's payment. In al-Quniyya: There is an issue because intention is a condition and it is not present from him. In al-Majma': And for a grazing animal whose owner refuses to pay it without his consent, we order him to pay it voluntarily. The fatwa is the detail: if it is from apparent wealth, then the obligation falls from its owners by the taking of the ruler or his deputy because he has authority to take; thereafter, if the ruler does not place it in its proper place, his taking does not nullify it. But if it is from hidden wealth, then the obligation does not fall because the ruler has no authority to take zakat of hidden wealth, so his taking is not valid. Thus in al-Tajnis, al-Waqi'at, and al-Walwaljiyya.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of zakat payment when the imam (ruler) takes it forcibly from a reluctant owner. It is a ruling established by proof: the imam has general authority (wilaya) to collect zakat from apparent wealth (amwal zahira) such as livestock and crops, as this is his right to enforce the obligation. The commentary notes a potential problem regarding intention (niyya), which is a condition for zakat validity, but the ruling is that the imam's taking suffices because his authority substitutes for the owner's action. The final fatwa distinguishes between apparent and hidden wealth: for apparent wealth, the ruler's taking suffices even if he misplaces the funds; for hidden wealth (e.g., gold, silver, trade goods), the ruler may not take forcibly because he lacks authority over it, and such taking does not fulfill the obligation. This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
ولاية (wilāya): Legal authority of the imam to collect and distribute zakat from certain assets.
أموال ظاهرة (amwāl ẓāhira): Visible assets like livestock and crops, for which the imam may enforce collection.
أموال باطنة (amwāl bāṭina): Hidden assets like gold, silver, and trade goods, where the owner's intention is necessary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi, if the imam forcibly takes zakat on hidden wealth (amwal batina) from an unwilling owner, what is the ruling?
A) The owner's obligation is fulfilled regardless.
B) The owner's obligation is not fulfilled, and he must still pay zakat.
C) The imam's action is valid only if he distributes it properly.
D) The owner must repeat the payment after the imam's taking.

Key Takeaway
The imam's forcible taking of zakat discharges the obligation only for apparent wealth due to his authority; for hidden wealth, the owner's duty remains.

Paying Zakat by Forgiving Debt: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute and the Legal Device
Ruling with expansion and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on discharging zakat by forgiving a debt, distinguish between the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding partial giving, and apply the legal device (ḥīla) to combine zakat and debt repayment.

Core Idea
Forgiving a debt of a poor person discharges zakat; partial forgiveness drops zakat only on that part, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ on the remainder.

This lesson explores a nuanced area of Hanafi zakat law: can you discharge your zakat obligation by simply forgiving a debt owed to you by a poor person? The text from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses this and also presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between the two leading disciples of Abu Hanifa regarding the effect of giving away part of the nisab. We will unpack the ruling, the disagreement, and a practical legal device (ḥīla) to resolve a conflict of principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Main Ruling on Full Forgiveness
Read the text: if a creditor forgives a debt owed by a poor person, his zakat is dropped even without intention. This is because the debt is considered as if collected.
Applies only if the debtor is poor.
2
Note the Condition for Partial Forgiveness
If the creditor forgives part of the debt, only the zakat on that part drops; the remaining debt still incurs zakat.
Even if he intended it as zakat for the remainder.
3
Understand the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Partial Giving
The text mentions a disagreement: if someone gives away part of his nisab (e.g., as a gift), Muhammad says the zakat on that portion drops; Abu Yusuf says it does not unless the amount is exactly 196 dirhams (the nisab for silver).
This dispute is about giving away part of the nisab, not specifically about forgiving debt.
4
Examine the Principle (Talil) of Payment in Kind
The text states: paying cash for cash or debt is valid; paying debt for cash or a receivable debt is invalid; paying debt for a debt not to be received is valid. This explains why forgiving a debt discharges zakat (it's like paying a debt that will not be received).
This principle underpins the ruling.
5
Apply the Legal Device (Hīla)
The hīla: the debtor gives the poor person five dirhams as zakat, then takes it back as repayment of a loan. This allows the creditor to give zakat and recover the debt simultaneously.
This is a permissible stratagem in Hanafi law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بالتصدق بالكل لأنه لو تصدق ببعض النصاب بلا نية اتفقوا أنه لا يسقط زكاة كله، واختلفوا في سقوط زكاة ما تصدق به فقال محمد بسقوطه، وقال أبو يوسف عليه زكاة كله إلا إذا كان الموهوب مائة وستة وتسعين فحينئذ تسقط . كذا في المبتغى بالغين المعجمة . وأطلق في التصدق بالكل فشمل العين والدين فلو كان له على فقير دين فأبرأه عنه سقط زكاته عنه ، نوى الزكاة أو لم ينو لما قدمناه ولو أبرأه عن البعض سقط زكاة ذلك البعض ولا تسقط عنه زكاة الباقي ولو نوى به الأداء عن الباقي لأن الباقي يصير عيناً بالقبض فيصير مؤدياً الدين عن العين والأصل فيه أن أداء العين عن العين وعن الدين يجوز، وأداء الدين عن العين وعن دين سيقبض لا يجوز، وأداء الدين عن دين لا يقبض يجوز . كذا في شرح الطحاوي. وحيلة الجواز أن يعطى المديون الفقير خمسة زكاة ثم يأخذها منه قضاء عن دينه . كذا في المحيط .
And it is conditioned by giving away all [the nisab], because if he gives away part of the nisab without intention, they agree that it does not drop the zakat of all of it. And they differed regarding dropping the zakat of what he gave away: Muhammad said it drops; Abu Yusuf said upon him is zakat of all of it, except if the gifted amount is one hundred and ninety-six, then it drops. Thus in al-Mubtagha (with ghayn mu'jama). And he [the author] gave absolute ruling regarding giving away all, so it includes cash ('ayn) and debt (dayn). So if he has a debt upon a poor person and he discharges him from it, his zakat is dropped from him, whether he intended zakat or not, according to what we have presented. And if he discharges him from part, the zakat of that part is dropped, and the zakat of the remaining part does not drop, even if he intends payment for the remaining, because the remaining becomes cash upon receipt, so he becomes one paying debt with cash. The principle is that paying cash for cash and for debt is permissible; paying debt for cash and for debt to be received is not permissible; and paying debt for debt not to be received is permissible. Thus in Sharh al-Tahawi. And the trick (hila) of permissibility is that the debtor gives the poor person five [dirhams] as zakat, then takes it from him as payment for his debt. Thus in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of paying zakat by giving away the entire nisab (the minimum wealth threshold) or by forgiving a debt. It begins by noting a condition: if someone gives away only part of his nisab without intention, all agree that his total zakat obligation does not drop. However, there is a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the zakat on the amount given away: Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that the zakat on that portion drops, while Abu Yusuf says zakat is due on the entire nisab unless the gifted amount is exactly 196 dirhams (which is the nisab for silver). The text then expands to the case of forgiving a debt: if a creditor forgives a debt owed by a poor person, his zakat liability is discharged whether or not he intended zakat, because the debt is considered as if it were paid. If he forgives part, only the zakat of that part drops. The text also explains the principle of payment in kind: paying cash (ayn) for cash or debt is valid, but paying debt for cash or a receivable debt is invalid. A practical hila (legal device) is given: the debtor gives the poor person five dirhams as zakat, then takes it back as repayment of the loan.

Key Terms
إبراء (ibrā'): Legal discharge from a debt; forgiving a debt owed by another.
حيلة الجواز (ḥīlat al-jawāz): A legal device to achieve a permissible result while avoiding a prohibition or difficulty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Cash can only be paid for cash, not debt.
B) Paying debt for a debt that is not to be received is permissible.
C) Paying debt for cash is always permissible.
D) Intention is required for any zakat payment.

Key Takeaway
Forgiving a debt of a poor person fulfills zakat; partial forgiveness drops zakat only on that part, and a hila allows combining zakat and debt repayment.

Intention and Conditions for Zakat Payment
Rulings on intention and conditions for zakat payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules regarding intention in zakat payment and identify which conditions (recipient knowledge, payment source) are required for validity.

Core Idea
Intention in the heart suffices for zakat; recipient need not know, and payment from other wealth is valid.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq deals with the role of intention (niyya) in zakat and clarifies several conditions. Many assume the recipient must be told it is zakat, but the Hanafi school holds that the giver's inner intention is sufficient. The text also addresses payment from different sources, including the case of paying from 'bad' wealth, and provides an analogy from wine sale to support the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling on intention
Focus on the opening: if a person intends nothing other than zakat, or intends voluntary charity (tatawwu'), the zakat obligation drops only according to Imam Muhammad's position. The correct view is that intention for zakat is necessary.
The text distinguishes between paying with no intention, wrong intention, or intention of another obligation.
2
Analyze the condition of recipient knowledge
The author did not require the recipient to know the payment is zakat. The text states there is disagreement but the correct view (al-sahih) from al-Mubtagha and al-Quniyya is that the giver's intention suffices, even if he calls it a gift or loan.
This is a legitimate intra-Hanafi difference; the relied-upon position is that knowledge is not required.
3
Examine the condition of payment source
The text clarifies that one need not pay from the exact zakat wealth. Payment from other funds is valid, as proven by the earlier ruling about ordering someone else to pay. There is a dispute about paying from 'bad' wealth, but the preferred view (from al-Quniyya) is that it suffices.
The wine analogy supports the validity of using proceeds from a dhimmi's sale of wine for zakat.
4
Synthesize the practical implications
Combine the rules: intention is key; the recipient's knowledge is irrelevant; payment can be from any lawful wealth, even if not the specific zakatable assets.
This gives flexibility in discharging the obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشمل أيضاً ما إذا لم ينو شيئاً أصلاً أو نوى غير الزكاة وهو الصحيح فيما إذا نوى التطوع، أما إذا تصدق بكله ناوياً النذر أو واجباً آخر فإنه يقع عما نوى ويضمن قدر الواجب . كذا في التبيين . وفي شرح الطحاوي لو وجبت الزكاة في مائتي درهم فأدى خمسة ونوى ذلك تطوعاً سقطت عنه زكاة الخمسة وهي ثمن درهم ولا تسقط عنه زكاة الباقي اهـ. وينبغي أن يكون مفرعاً على قول محمد كما لا يخفى. ولم يشترط المصنف رحمه الله علم الآخذ بما يأخذه أنه زكاة للإشارة إلى أنه ليس بشرط وفيه اختلاف والأصح كما في المبتغى والقنية أن من أعطى مسكيناً دراهم وسماها هبة أو قرضاً ونوى الزكاة فإنها تجزئه، ولم يشترط أيضاً الدفع من عين مال الزكاة لما قدمناه أنه لو أمر إنساناً بالدفع من أجزأه لكن اختلف فيما إذا دفع من مال آخر خبيث. وظاهر القنية ترجيح الإجزاء استدلالاً بقولهم : مسلم له خمر فوكل ذميا فباعها من ذمي فللمسلم أن يصرف هذا الثمن إلى الفقراء من زكاة ماله .اهـ.
And it also includes the case when he intends nothing at all or intends other than zakat—and the correct is that when he intends voluntary charity (tatawwu'), then if he gives all of it intending a vow (nadhr) or other obligation, it falls upon what he intended and he is liable for the amount of the obligation. Thus in al-Tabyin. In Sharh al-Tahawi: If zakat becomes obligatory on two hundred dirhams, and he pays five and intends that as a voluntary charity, the zakat on those five (which is one-eighth of a dirham) drops, and the zakat on the remainder does not drop. And it should be derived from Muhammad's position, as is not hidden. And the author (may Allah have mercy on him) did not condition that the recipient know that what he takes is zakat, to indicate that it is not a condition. There is disagreement, but the correct view, as in al-Mubtagha and al-Quniyya, is that one who gives dirhams to a poor person and calls it a gift or loan, but intends it as zakat, it suffices him. And he also did not condition payment from the very same zakat wealth, because of what we have presented: if he orders someone to pay from it, it suffices. But they differed when he pays from other, inferior wealth. The apparent view in al-Quniyya is preference for sufficiency, basing it on their statement: A Muslim has wine, so he appoints a dhimmi to sell it to another dhimmi, then the Muslim may disburse that price to the poor as zakat from his wealth.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage addresses the role of intention (niyya) in zakat payment. It covers several scenarios: (1) If a person gives zakat money without any intention (e.g., just giving away money) or intends something other than zakat (e.g., voluntary charity), the ruling depends on what he intended. If he intended voluntary charity, the zakat obligation on that amount does not drop unless he intended zakat. However, if he gave the entire nisab intending a different obligation (like a vow), it counts for that obligation but he must still pay zakat. (2) The text then discusses that the recipient does not need to know that the payment is zakat; it is sufficient if the giver intends it. This is the correct view according to al-Mubtagha and al-Quniyya. (3) Furthermore, it is not required that the payment be made from the specific zakat wealth (ayn mal al-zakat); one can pay from other funds. There is a dispute regarding paying from 'bad' (lower quality) wealth, but the preferred view is that it suffices, analogous to the case of a Muslim who appoints a dhimmi to sell wine (which is permissible for the dhimmi) and uses the proceeds for zakat.

Key Terms
نية (niyya): Inner intention to perform zakat as an obligatory act; essential for validity.
تطوع (tatawwu'): Voluntary charity (nafl) as opposed to obligatory zakat.
خبيث (khabith): Inferior, bad, or impure (wealth that is not genuinely lawful, e.g., acquired through impermissible means but owner repented).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person gives a poor man 50 dirhams, saying 'this is a gift', but in his heart intends it as zakat. According to the correct view (al-sahih), does this discharge his zakat obligation?
A) Yes, because intention in the heart is sufficient.
B) No, because he verbally said it was a gift.
C) Yes, but only if the poor man knows it is zakat.
D) No, because he must explicitly say it is zakat.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of zakat payment hinges on the giver's inner intention (niyya); explicit declaration to the recipient and payment from the specific zakat wealth are not required.
